# Text Elements
CAPACITORS ^zE44ORRC


A capacitor is a passive element designed to store energy in its electric field.
Besides resistors, capacitors are the most common electrical component. Capacitors
are used extensively in electronics, communications, computers, and power systems. For
example, they are used in tuning circuits of radio receivers and as dynamic memory 
elements in a computer.
 ^qYdlZAGb

A capacitor is typically constructed as depicted below in fig 1 ^EHppRE8f

Metal plates, 
each with area A ^PfebnD5J

Dielectric with
permittivity ^EjKccyUq

A CAPACITOR consists of two conducting plates separated by an insulator (or dielectric) ^34mDtjnB

When a voltage source v is connected to the capacitor, as shown above, the source deposits a positive charge
q on one plate and a negative charge -q on the other. The capacitor is said to store the electric charge. The 
amount of charge stored, represented by q, is directly proportional to the applied voltage v so that: ^dxQ6W9Q6

where C, the constant of proportionality, is known as the capacitance of the capacitor. It has
unit of farad (F) ^gFXunlJs

Capacitance is the ratio of the charge on one plate of the capacitor to the voltage difference between the
two plates, measured in farads (F) ^hBuZ6EXE

NOTE: 1 farad = 1 coulomb/volt ^C08QSTnr

Although the capacitance C of a capacitor is the ratio of the charge q per plate to the total applied voltage v.
It depends on the physical dimensions of the capacitor. For example, for the parallel-plate capacitor shown above, the
capacitance is given by: ^NZ7SMqfX

   ------- (ii) ^McAxp1V8

   ------- (i) ^95AUZoKA

Where: 
- A is the surface area of each plate
- d is the distance between the plates
-   is the permittivity of the dielectric material between the plates ^qssfldJr

Equation ii applies only to parallel-plate capacitors, but we may infer from it that, in general, three factors determine
the value of capacitance:

1.  The surface area of the plates - the larger the area, the greater the capacitance.
2. The spacing between the plates - the smaller the spacing, the greater the capacitance.
3. The permittivity of the material - the higher the permittivity, the greater the capacitance.

Equation ix or x represents the energy stored in the electric field that exists between the plates of the capacitor. This
energy can be retrieved since an ideal capacitor CANNOT DISSIPATE ENERGY. In fact, the word CAPACITOR is derived
from this element's capacity to store energy in an electric field.
    We should note the following important properties of a capacitor:
1.  Note from equation iv that when the voltage across a capacitor is not changing with time (ie., dc voltage), the current 
   through the capacitor is zero. Thus,
            
                                    A CAPACITOR IS AN OPEN CIRCUIT TO DC
    
    However, if a battery (dc voltage) is connected across a capacitor, the capacitor charges.

2.  The voltage on the capacitor must be continuous.

                              THE VOLTAGE ON A CAPACITOR CANNOT CHANGE ABRUPTLY.

3.  The ideal capacitor does not dissipate energy. It takes power from the circuit when storing energy in its electric field
    and returns previously stored energy when delivering power to the circuit.

4.  A real, non-ideal capacitor has a parallel-model leakage resistance, as shown in fig iii. The leakage resistance may be as
    high as 100M ohms and can be neglected for most practical applications. ^triVkGc4

INDUCTORS ^bsiaEuYO

An inductor is a passive element designed to store energy in its magnetic field. Inductors find numerous applications
in electronic and power systems. They are used for power supplies, transformers, radios, TV's, radars and electric 
motors.
    Any conductor of electric current has inductive properties and many can be regarded as an inductor. But in
order to enhance the inductive effect, a practical inductor is usually formed into a cylinder coil with many turns of
conducting wires ^klpti7R8

An inductor consists of a coil of conducting wire ^GuqClmfp

If current is allowed to pass through an inductor, it is found that the voltage across the inductor is directly
proportional to the time rate of change of the current. Using the passive sign convection: ^W5JQkl2l

------ (i) ^3aXsS3wQ

Where L is the constant of proportionality called the INDUCTANCE of the inductor. The unit of inductance is
henry (H)

     INDUCTANCE IS THE PROPERTY WHEREBY AN INDUCTOR EXHIBITS OPPOSITION TO THE CHANGE
     OF CURRENT FLOWING THROUGH IT, MEASURED IN HENRY (H). ^AlPDZ5oL

The inductance of an inductor depends on its physical dimension and construction. Formulas for calculating the 
inductance of inductors of different shapes are derived from electromagnetic theory and can be found in standard
electrical engineering books. For example, for the inductor, (solenoid), we have: ^19Er0D7x

------ (ii) ^jsVfcp7v

Where N is the number of turns, l is the length, A is the cross-sectional area, and   is the permeability of the core ^42mi7VmW

Equation i is the voltage-current relationship for an inductor. For an inductor whose inductance is independent of
current. Such an inductor is known as a LINEAR INDUCTOR. For a NONLINEAR INDUCTOR, the plot of equation
i will not be a straight line. Because inductance varies with current.

    The current-voltage relationship is obtained from equation i as: ^3qdXMcBu

Integrating give: ^AkF4ds0W

OR ^7GnBK02j

The energy stored is: ^gdyXypCD

We should note the following important properties of an inductor:

1.  Note form equation (i) that the voltage across an inductor is zero when the current is constant. Thus,
                
                AN INDUCTOR ACTS LIKE A SHORT CIRCUIT TO DC

2.  An important property of the inductor is its opposition to the change in current flowing through it

                THE CURRENT THROUGH AN INDUCTOR CANNOT CHANGE INSTANTANEOUSLY.

3.  Like an ideal capacitor, the ideal inductor does not dissipate energy. The energy stored in it can be 
    retrieved at a later time. The inductor takes power from the circuit when storing energy and delivers 
    power to the circuit when returning previously stored energy.

4.  A practical non-ideal inductor has a significant resistive component. This is due to the fact that the 
    inductor is made of a conducting material such as copper, which has some resistance. This resistance is
    called winding resistance and it appears in series with the inductance of the inductor. The presence of this
    winding resistance  makes it both an energy storage device and an energy dissipation device. Since the 
    winding resistance is usually very small, it is ignored in most cases. The nonlinear inductor also has a 
    winding capacitance due to the capacitive coupling between the conducting coils. This winding capacitance
    is very small and can be ignored in most cases, except at hight frequencies. ^feTL5rip

FIRST ORDER CIRCUITS ^1h6YpAwQ

A first order circuit is characterized by a first-order differential equation ^YpsDqsnM

In addition to there being two types of first order circuits (RC and RL), there are two ways to excite the
circuits. The first way is by initial conditions of the storage element in the circuit. In these so-called
SOURCE FREE CIRCUITS, we assume that energy is initially stored in the capacitive or inductive element.
The energy causes current to flow in the circuit and is gradually dissipated in the resistor, the second
way of exciting first order-order circuits is by independent sources. ^gF4SpWR3

First order circuits have a single storage element, this can either be a capacitor or an inductor ^FuXCgLpW

THE SOURCE FREE RC CIRCUIT ^l0hsk9lv

A source-free RC circuit occurs when its dc source is suddenly disconnected,
the energy already stored in the capacitor is releases to the resistor. Our
objective is to determine the circuit response, which, for pedagogic reasons, we
assume to be the voltage v(t) across the capacitor. Since the capacitor is 
initially charges, we can assume that at time t = 0, the initial voltage is ^qKKVzENu

A circuit response is the manner
in which the circuit reacts to an
excitation ^9i7FukJy

This shows that the voltage response of the RC circuit is an
exponential decay of the initial voltage. Since the response is due
to the initial energy stored and the physical characteristics of the
circuit and not due to some external voltage or current source, it is
called the NORMAL RESPONSE OF THE CIRCUIT ^6ex4c0rL

The NATURAL RESPONSE of
a circuit refers to the behavior
(in terms of voltages and currents)
of the circuit itself, with no external
sources of excitation. ^3zADRDqD

The natural response depends on the
nature of the circuit alone, with no
external sources. In fact, the circuit
has a response only because of the 
energy initially stored in the capacitor ^7sbvPdEl

time required for the response to decay
to a factor of 1/e or 36.8 percent of its
initial value ^8LWsjemv

From the table, it evident that the voltage v(t) is less than 1 percent of the initial value after 5    . Thus , it is customary
to assume that the capacitor is fully discharged (or charged) after five time constants. In other words, it takes
five time constants for the circuit to reach it's final state or steady state when no changes take place with time.
Note that for every time interval of   , the voltage is reduced gy 36.8 percent of its previous value.

    REMARK: the smaller the time constant, the more rapidly the voltage decreases, that is the faster the response
A circuit with a faster response reaches it's steady state quickly due to quick dissipation of energy stored, where as a 
circuit with a large time constant gives a slow response because it takes longer to reach steady state. ^5eBKhCgl

SECOND-ORDER CIRCUITS ^RnjXa55Q

    In this chapter, we will consider circuits containing two storage elements. These are known as
SECOND-ORDER circuits because their responses are described by differential equations that 
contain second derivatives.
    Typical examples of second-order circuits are RLC circuits, in which the three kinds of passive
elements are present. Examples of such circuits are shown by the right. Other examples are RL and
RC circuits. It is apparent that a second order circuit may have two storage elements of different 
types or the same type (provided elements of the same type cannot be represented by an equivalent
single element) ^ToA7xo8L

A SECOND-ORDER CIRCUIT IS CHARACTERIZED BY A SECOND ORDER DIFFERENTIAL EQUATION.
IT CONSISTS OF RESISTORS AND THE EQUIVALENT OF TWO ENERGY STORAGE ELEMENTS ^oxJQHQxS

FINDING INITIAL AND FINAL VALUES ^9SjNAnpL

Perhaps the major problem students face in handling second-order circuits is finding the initial and final conditions on 
circuit variables. Students are usually comfortable getting the initial and final values of v and i but often have
difficulty finding the initial values of their derivatives: dv/dt and di/dt. For this reason, this section is explicitly
devoted to the subtleties of getting v(0), i(0), dv(0)/dt, di(0)/dt, i(infinty) and v(infinity).
NOTE: Unless otherwise stated, v denotes the capacitor voltage and i is the inductor current. ^sGQXLUhm

There are two key points to keep in mind in determining the initial conditions.

First-> as always in circuit analysis we must carefully handle the polarity of voltage v(t) across the capacitor
and the direction of current i(t) through the inductor. Keep in mind that v and i are defined strictly according
to sign convection. One should carefully observe how these are defined and apply them accordingly. 
   
Secondly-> keep in mind that the capacitor voltage is always continuous so that:



and the inductor current is always continuous so that ^cQmJhbhh

where      denotes the time just before a switching event and      is the time just after the switching event,
assuming that the switching event takes place at  ^ur6cllND

    Thus in finding initial conditions, we first focus on those variables that cannot change abruptly ^aKlUYbHn

			"text": "Equation ix or x represents the energy stored in the electric field that exists between the plates of the capacitor. This\nenergy can be retrieved since an ideal capacitor CANNOT DISSIPATE ENERGY. In fact, the word CAPACITOR is derived\nfrom this element's capacity to store energy in an electric field.\n    We should note the following important properties of a capacitor:\n1.  Note from equation iv that when the voltage across a capacitor is not changing with time (ie., dc voltage), the current \n   through the capacitor is zero. Thus,\n            \n                                    A CAPACITOR IS AN OPEN CIRCUIT TO DC\n    \n    However, if a battery (dc voltage) is connected across a capacitor, the capacitor charges.\n\n2.  The voltage on the capacitor must be continuous.\n\n                              THE VOLTAGE ON A CAPACITOR CANNOT CHANGE ABRUPTLY.\n\n3.  The ideal capacitor does not dissipate energy. It takes power from the circuit when storing energy in its electric field\n    and returns previously stored energy when delivering power to the circuit.\n\n4.  A real, non-ideal capacitor has a parallel-model leakage resistance, as shown in fig iii. The leakage resistance may be as\n    high as 100M ohms and can be neglected for most practical applications.",
			"rawText": "Equation ix or x represents the energy stored in the electric field that exists between the plates of the capacitor. This\nenergy can be retrieved since an ideal capacitor CANNOT DISSIPATE ENERGY. In fact, the word CAPACITOR is derived\nfrom this element's capacity to store energy in an electric field.\n    We should note the following important properties of a capacitor:\n1.  Note from equation iv that when the voltage across a capacitor is not changing with time (ie., dc voltage), the current \n   through the capacitor is zero. Thus,\n            \n                                    A CAPACITOR IS AN OPEN CIRCUIT TO DC\n    \n    However, if a battery (dc voltage) is connected across a capacitor, the capacitor charges.\n\n2.  The voltage on the capacitor must be continuous.\n\n                              THE VOLTAGE ON A CAPACITOR CANNOT CHANGE ABRUPTLY.\n\n3.  The ideal capacitor does not dissipate energy. It takes power from the circuit when storing energy in its electric field\n    and returns previously stored energy when delivering power to the circuit.\n\n4.  A real, non-ideal capacitor has a parallel-model leakage resistance, as shown in fig iii. The leakage resistance may be as\n    high as 100M ohms and can be neglected for most practical applications.",

			"text": "We should note the following important properties of an inductor:\n\n1.  Note form equation (i) that the voltage across an inductor is zero when the current is constant. Thus,\n                \n                AN INDUCTOR ACTS LIKE A SHORT CIRCUIT TO DC\n\n2.  An important property of the inductor is its opposition to the change in current flowing through it\n\n                THE CURRENT THROUGH AN INDUCTOR CANNOT CHANGE INSTANTANEOUSLY.\n\n3.  Like an ideal capacitor, the ideal inductor does not dissipate energy. The energy stored in it can be \n    retrieved at a later time. The inductor takes power from the circuit when storing energy and delivers \n    power to the circuit when returning previously stored energy.\n\n4.  A practical non-ideal inductor has a significant resistive component. This is due to the fact that the \n    inductor is made of a conducting material such as copper, which has some resistance. This resistance is\n    called winding resistance and it appears in series with the inductance of the inductor. The presence of this\n    winding resistance  makes it both an energy storage device and an energy dissipation device. Since the \n    winding resistance is usually very small, it is ignored in most cases. The nonlinear inductor also has a \n    winding capacitance due to the capacitive coupling between the conducting coils. This winding capacitance\n    is very small and can be ignored in most cases, except at hight frequencies.",
			"rawText": "We should note the following important properties of an inductor:\n\n1.  Note form equation (i) that the voltage across an inductor is zero when the current is constant. Thus,\n                \n                AN INDUCTOR ACTS LIKE A SHORT CIRCUIT TO DC\n\n2.  An important property of the inductor is its opposition to the change in current flowing through it\n\n                THE CURRENT THROUGH AN INDUCTOR CANNOT CHANGE INSTANTANEOUSLY.\n\n3.  Like an ideal capacitor, the ideal inductor does not dissipate energy. The energy stored in it can be \n    retrieved at a later time. The inductor takes power from the circuit when storing energy and delivers \n    power to the circuit when returning previously stored energy.\n\n4.  A practical non-ideal inductor has a significant resistive component. This is due to the fact that the \n    inductor is made of a conducting material such as copper, which has some resistance. This resistance is\n    called winding resistance and it appears in series with the inductance of the inductor. The presence of this\n    winding resistance  makes it both an energy storage device and an energy dissipation device. Since the \n    winding resistance is usually very small, it is ignored in most cases. The nonlinear inductor also has a \n    winding capacitance due to the capacitive coupling between the conducting coils. This winding capacitance\n    is very small and can be ignored in most cases, except at hight frequencies.",

			"originalText": "We should note the following important properties of an inductor:\n\n1.  Note form equation (i) that the voltage across an inductor is zero when the current is constant. Thus,\n                \n                AN INDUCTOR ACTS LIKE A SHORT CIRCUIT TO DC\n\n2.  An important property of the inductor is its opposition to the change in current flowing through it\n\n                THE CURRENT THROUGH AN INDUCTOR CANNOT CHANGE INSTANTANEOUSLY.\n\n3.  Like an ideal capacitor, the ideal inductor does not dissipate energy. The energy stored in it can be \n    retrieved at a later time. The inductor takes power from the circuit when storing energy and delivers \n    power to the circuit when returning previously stored energy.\n\n4.  A practical non-ideal inductor has a significant resistive component. This is due to the fact that the \n    inductor is made of a conducting material such as copper, which has some resistance. This resistance is\n    called winding resistance and it appears in series with the inductance of the inductor. The presence of this\n    winding resistance  makes it both an energy storage device and an energy dissipation device. Since the \n    winding resistance is usually very small, it is ignored in most cases. The nonlinear inductor also has a \n    winding capacitance due to the capacitive coupling between the conducting coils. This winding capacitance\n    is very small and can be ignored in most cases, except at hight frequencies.",
